<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Logic</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Study of correct reasoning</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the study of correct reasoning. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Logic_(disambiguation)" title="Logic (disambiguation)">Logic (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Logician" redirects here. For the school of Chinese philosophy, see <a href="/wiki/School_of_Names" title="School of Names">School of Names</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modus_ponendo_ponens.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="562" data-file-width="682" decoding="async" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Modus_ponendo_ponens.png/220px-Modus_ponendo_ponens.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Modus_ponendo_ponens.png/330px-Modus_ponendo_ponens.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Modus_ponendo_ponens.png/440px-Modus_ponendo_ponens.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modus_ponendo_ponens.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Logic studies valid forms of inference like the <a href="/wiki/Modus_ponens" title="Modus ponens">modus ponens</a>.</div></div></div>

<img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/>Â <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Philosophy_sidebar" title="Template talk:Philosophy sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Philosophy_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Logic</b> is the study of correct <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reasoning</a>. It includes both <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">formal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Informal_logic" title="Informal logic">informal logic</a>. Formal logic is the science of <a href="/wiki/Validity_(logic)" title="Validity (logic)">deductively valid</a> inferences or of <a href="/wiki/Logical_truth" title="Logical truth">logical truths</a>. It is a <a href="/wiki/Formal_science" title="Formal science">formal science</a> investigating how conclusions follow from <a href="/wiki/Premise" title="Premise">premises</a> in a topic-neutral way. When used as a countable noun, the term "a logic" refers to a logical <a href="/wiki/Formal_system" title="Formal system">formal system</a> that articulates a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proof_system" title="Proof system">proof system</a>. Formal logic contrasts with informal logic, which is associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Informal_fallacies" title="Informal fallacies">informal fallacies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Critical_thinking" title="Critical thinking">critical thinking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Argumentation_theory" title="Argumentation theory">argumentation theory</a>. While there is no general agreement on how formal and informal logic are to be distinguished, one prominent approach associates their difference with whether the studied <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">arguments</a> are expressed in <a href="/wiki/Formal_language" title="Formal language">formal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">informal languages</a>. Logic plays a central role in multiple fields, such as philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>.
</p><p>Logic studies arguments, which consist of a set of premises together with a conclusion. Premises and conclusions are usually understood either as <a href="/wiki/Sentence_(mathematical_logic)" title="Sentence (mathematical logic)">sentences</a> or as <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">propositions</a> and are characterized by their internal structure; complex propositions are made up of simpler propositions linked to each other by <a href="/wiki/Logical_connective" title="Logical connective">propositional connectives</a> like <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \land }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \land }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\land " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d6823e5a222eb3ca49672818ac3d13ec607052c4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.55ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> (and) or <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \to }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mo stretchy="false">â<!-- â --></mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \to }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\to " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1daab843254cfcb23a643070cf93f3badc4fbbbd" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.324ex; height:1.843ex;"/></span> (if...then). The truth of a proposition usually depends on the <a href="/wiki/Denotation" title="Denotation">denotations</a> of its constituents. Logically true propositions constitute a special case since their truth depends only on the logical vocabulary used in them and not on the denotations of other terms. 
</p><p>Arguments can be either correct or incorrect. An argument is correct if its premises support its conclusion. The strongest form of support is found in <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deductive arguments</a>: it is impossible for their premises to be true and their conclusion to be false. Deductive arguments contrast with <a href="/wiki/Ampliative" title="Ampliative">ampliative</a> arguments, which may arrive in their conclusion at new information that is not present in the premises. However, it is possible for all their premises to be true while their conclusion is still false. Many arguments found in everyday discourse and the sciences are ampliative arguments, sometimes divided into <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abductive_reasoning" title="Abductive reasoning">abductive</a> arguments. Inductive arguments usually take the form of <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistical</a> generalizations while abductive arguments are <i><a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inferences</a> to the best explanation</i>. Arguments that fall short of the standards of correct reasoning are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fallacies" title="Fallacies">fallacies</a>.
</p><p>Systems of logic are theoretical frameworks for assessing the correctness of reasoning and arguments. Logic has been studied since <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">Antiquity</a>; early approaches include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotelian_logic" title="Aristotelian logic">Aristotelian logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoic_logic" title="Stoic logic">Stoic logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anviksiki" title="Anviksiki">Anviksiki</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohists" title="Mohists">mohists</a>. Modern formal logic has its roots in the work of late 19th-century mathematicians such as <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>. While Aristotelian logic focuses on reasoning in the form of <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogisms</a>, in the modern era its traditional dominance was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Classical_logic" title="Classical logic">classical logic</a>, a set of fundamental logical intuitions shared by most logicians. It consists of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propositional_logic" title="Propositional logic">propositional logic</a>, which only considers the logical relations on the level of propositions, and <a href="/wiki/First-order_logic" title="First-order logic">first-order logic</a>, which also articulates the internal structure of propositions using various linguistic devices, such as predicates and <a href="/wiki/Quantifier_(logic)" title="Quantifier (logic)">quantifiers</a>. Extended logics accept the basic intuitions behind classical logic and extend it to other fields, such as <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>. Deviant logics, on the other hand, reject certain classical intuitions and provide alternative accounts of the fundamental laws of logic.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The word "logic" originates from the Greek word "logos", which has a variety of translations, such as <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, <a href="/wiki/Discourse" title="Discourse">discourse</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Logic is traditionally defined as the study of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laws_of_thought" title="Laws of thought">laws of thought</a> or correct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup> and is usually understood in terms of <a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inferences</a> or <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">arguments</a>. Reasoning may be seen as the activity of drawing inferences whose outward expression is given in arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup> An inference or an argument is a set of premises together with a conclusion. Logic is interested in whether arguments are good or inferences are valid, i.e. whether the premises support their conclusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKeon_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKeon-8">[8]</a></sup> These general characterizations apply to logic in the widest sense since they are true both for formal and informal logic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blair_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blair-9">[9]</a></sup> but many definitions of logic focus on the more paradigmatic formal logic. In this narrower sense, logic is a formal science that studies how conclusions follow from premises in a topic-neutral way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacFarlane2_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacFarlane2-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corkum_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corkum-11">[11]</a></sup> In this regard, logic is sometimes contrasted with the theory of <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a>, which is wider since it covers all forms of good reasoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford-logic-onthology_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-logic-onthology-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlfredTarski1968.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="271" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/AlfredTarski1968.jpeg/250px-AlfredTarski1968.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/AlfredTarski1968.jpeg/375px-AlfredTarski1968.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/71/AlfredTarski1968.jpeg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlfredTarski1968.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alfred_Tarski" title="Alfred Tarski">Alfred Tarski</a> was an important defender of the idea that <a href="/wiki/Logical_truth" title="Logical truth">logical truth</a> can be defined in terms of possible interpretations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/Formal_science" title="Formal science">formal science</a>, logic contrasts with empirical sciences like <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> in that it tries to characterize the inferential relations between premises and conclusions based on their structure alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magnus_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Magnus-15">[15]</a></sup> This means that the actual content of these propositions, i.e. their specific topic, is not important for whether the inference is valid or not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacFarlane2_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacFarlane2-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corkum_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Corkum-11">[11]</a></sup> Valid inferences are characterized by the fact that the truth of their premises ensures the truth of their conclusion: it is impossible for the premises to be true and the conclusion to be false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKeon_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-McKeon-8">[8]</a></sup> The general logical structures characterizing valid inferences are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rules_of_inference" title="Rules of inference">rules of inference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup> In this sense, logic is often defined as the study of valid inference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup> This contrasts with another prominent characterization of logic as the science of <a href="/wiki/Logical_truth" title="Logical truth">logical truths</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup> A proposition is logically true if its truth depends only on the logical vocabulary used in it. This means that it is true in all <a href="/wiki/Possible_world" title="Possible world">possible worlds</a> and under all <a href="/wiki/Interpretation_(logic)" title="Interpretation (logic)">interpretations</a> of its non-logical terms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GÃ³mez-Torrente_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-GÃ³mez-Torrente-17">[17]</a></sup> These two characterizations of logic are closely related to each other: an inference is valid if the <a href="/wiki/Material_conditional" title="Material conditional">material conditional</a> from its premises to its conclusion is logically true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term "logic" can also be used in a slightly different sense as a countable noun. In this sense, <i>a logic</i> is a logical <a href="/wiki/Formal_system" title="Formal system">formal system</a>. Different logics differ from each other concerning the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formal_languages" title="Formal languages">formal languages</a> used to express them and, most importantly, concerning the rules of inference they accept as valid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacquette_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquette-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup> Starting in the 20th century, many new formal systems have been proposed. There are various disagreements concerning what makes a formal system a logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacquette_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquette-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup> For example, it has been suggested that only <a href="/wiki/Completeness_(logic)" title="Completeness (logic)">logically complete</a> systems qualify as logics. For such reasons, some theorists deny that <a href="/wiki/Higher-order_logic" title="Higher-order logic">higher-order logics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_logic" title="Fuzzy logic">fuzzy logic</a> are logics in the strict sense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_and_informal_logic">Formal and informal logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Formal and informal logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Logic encompasses both formal and <a href="/wiki/Informal_logic" title="Informal logic">informal logic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup> Formal logic is the traditionally dominant field,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup> but applying its insights to actual everyday arguments has prompted modern developments of informal logic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInformal_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInformal-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambridgeInformal_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-CambridgeInformal-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-21">[21]</a></sup> which considers problems that formal logic on its own is unable to address.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-21">[21]</a></sup> Both provide criteria for assessing the correctness of arguments and distinguishing them from fallacies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blair_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blair-9">[9]</a></sup> Various suggestions have been made concerning how to draw the distinction between the two but there is no universally accepted answer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First-order_logic.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="2094" decoding="async" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/First-order_logic.png/350px-First-order_logic.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/First-order_logic.png/525px-First-order_logic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/First-order_logic.png/700px-First-order_logic.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First-order_logic.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Formal logic needs to translate natural language arguments into a formal language, like first-order logic, in order to assess whether they are valid. In this example, the colors indicate how the English words correspond to the symbols.</div></div></div>
<p>The most literal approach sees the terms "formal" and "informal" as applying to the language used to express arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OxfordInformal_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordInformal-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInformal_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInformal-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CambridgeInformal_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-CambridgeInformal-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-21">[21]</a></sup> On this view, formal logic studies arguments expressed in formal languages while informal logic studies arguments expressed in informal or <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">natural languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup> This means that the inference from the formulas <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>"</span> and <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8752c7023b4b3286800fe3238271bbca681219ed" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.838ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>"</span> to the conclusion <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P\land Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P\land Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P\land Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c5690bb4822d8c821a00cfe3c6644b046a884af4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.166ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>"</span> is studied by formal logic. The inference from the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> sentences "Al lit a cigarette" and "Bill stormed out of the room" to the sentence "Al lit a cigarette and Bill stormed out of the room", on the other hand, belongs to informal logic. Formal languages are characterized by their precision and simplicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OxfordInformal_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordInformal-23">[23]</a></sup> They normally contain a very limited vocabulary and exact rules on how their symbols can be used to construct sentences, usually referred to as <a href="/wiki/Well-formed_formula" title="Well-formed formula">well-formed formulas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalLanguage_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalLanguage-24">[24]</a></sup> This simplicity and exactness of formal logic make it capable of formulating precise rules of inference that determine whether a given argument is valid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalLanguage_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalLanguage-24">[24]</a></sup> This approach brings with it the need to translate natural language arguments into the formal language before their validity can be assessed, a procedure that comes with various problems of its own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magnus_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Magnus-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_21-4"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-21">[21]</a></sup> Informal logic avoids some of these problems by analyzing natural language arguments in their original form without the need of translation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInformal_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInformal-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blair_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Blair-9">[9]</a></sup> But it faces problems associated with the <a href="/wiki/Ambiguity" title="Ambiguity">ambiguity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vagueness" title="Vagueness">vagueness</a>, and context-dependence of natural language expressions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engel2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engel2-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_21-5"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-21">[21]</a></sup> A closely related approach applies the terms "formal" and "informal" not just to the language used, but more generally to the standards, criteria, and procedures of argumentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlairCurrent_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-BlairCurrent-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another approach draws the distinction according to the different types of inferences analyzed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weddle_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weddle-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInformal_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInformal-19">[19]</a></sup> This perspective understands formal logic as the study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deductive_inference" title="Deductive inference">deductive inferences</a> in contrast to informal logic as the study of non-deductive inferences, like <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive</a> or <a href="/wiki/Abductive_reasoning" title="Abductive reasoning">abductive inferences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInformal_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInformal-19">[19]</a></sup> The characteristic of deductive inferences is that the truth of their premises ensures the truth of their conclusion. This means that if all the premises are true, it is impossible for the conclusion to be false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKeon_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-McKeon-8">[8]</a></sup> For this reason, deductive inferences are in a sense trivial or uninteresting since they do not provide the thinker with any new information not already found in the premises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'Agostino_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-D'Agostino-29">[29]</a></sup> Non-deductive inferences, on the other hand, are <a href="/wiki/Ampliative" title="Ampliative">ampliative</a>: they help the thinker learn something above and beyond what is already stated in the premises. They achieve this at the cost of certainty: even if all premises are true, the conclusion of an ampliative argument may still be false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backmann_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Backmann-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPArguments_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPArguments-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>One more approach tries to link the difference between formal and informal logic to the distinction between <a href="/wiki/Formal_fallacy" title="Formal fallacy">formal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Informal_fallacies" title="Informal fallacies">informal fallacies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BlairCurrent_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-BlairCurrent-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_21-6"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInformal_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInformal-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup> This distinction is often drawn in relation to the <i><a href="/wiki/Logical_form" title="Logical form">form</a></i>, <i>content</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Context_(language_use)" title="Context (language use)">context</a></i> of arguments. In the case of formal fallacies, the error is found on the level of the argument's form, whereas for informal fallacies, the content and context of the argument are responsible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VleetIntro_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-VleetIntro-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dowden_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dowden-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stump_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stump-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korb_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Korb-36">[36]</a></sup> Formal logic abstracts away from the argument's content and is only interested in its form, specifically whether it follows a valid rule of inference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacFarlane2_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacFarlane2-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corkum_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Corkum-11">[11]</a></sup> In this regard, it is not important for the validity of a formal argument whether its premises are true or false. Informal logic, on the other hand, also takes the content and context of an argument into consideration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-7"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_21-7"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OxfordInformal_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-OxfordInformal-23">[23]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/False_dilemma" title="False dilemma">false dilemma</a>, for example, involves an error of content by excluding viable options, as in "you are either with us or against us; you are not with us; therefore, you are against us".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dowden_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dowden-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackie_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie-37">[37]</a></sup> For the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strawman_fallacy" title="Strawman fallacy">strawman fallacy</a>, on the other hand, the error is found on the level of context: a weak position is first described and then defeated, even though the opponent does not hold this position. But in another context, against an opponent that actually defends the strawman position, the argument is correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dowden_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dowden-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other accounts draw the distinction based on investigating general forms of arguments in contrast to particular instances or on the study of <a href="/wiki/Logical_constant" title="Logical constant">logical constants</a> instead of substantive <a href="/wiki/Concept" title="Concept">concepts</a>. A further approach focuses on the discussion of logical topics with or without formal devices or on the role of <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> for the assessment of arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-8"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_21-8"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fundamental_concepts">Fundamental concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Fundamental concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Premises.2C_conclusions.2C_and_truth"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Premises,_conclusions,_and_truth">Premises, conclusions, and truth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Premises, conclusions, and truth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Premises_and_conclusions">Premises and conclusions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Premises and conclusions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Premise" title="Premise">premise</a></div>
<p><i>Premises</i> and <i>conclusions</i> are the basic parts of inferences or arguments and therefore play a central role in logic. In the case of a valid inference or a correct argument, the conclusion follows from the premises, or in other words, the premises support the conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup> For instance, the premises "Mars is red" and "Mars is a planet" support the conclusion "Mars is a red planet". It is generally accepted that premises and conclusions have to be <a href="/wiki/Truth-bearer" title="Truth-bearer">truth-bearers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntvarguments_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-ntvarguments-39">[i]</a></sup> This means that they have a <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">truth value</a>: they are either true or false. Thus contemporary philosophy generally sees them either as <i><a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">propositions</a></i> or as <i><a href="/wiki/Sentence_(linguistics)" title="Sentence (linguistics)">sentences</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup> Propositions are the <a href="/wiki/Denotation" title="Denotation">denotations</a> of sentences and are usually understood as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abstract_object" title="Abstract object">abstract objects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Propositional theories of premises and conclusions are often criticized because of the difficulties involved in specifying the identity criteria of abstract objects or because of <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">naturalist</a> considerations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup> These objections are avoided by seeing premises and conclusions not as propositions but as sentences, i.e. as concrete linguistic objects like the symbols displayed on a page of a book. But this approach comes with new problems of its own: sentences are often context-dependent and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambiguous" title="Ambiguous">ambiguous</a>, meaning an argument's validity would not only depend on its parts but also on its context and on how it is interpreted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pietroski_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pietroski-41">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>In earlier work, premises and conclusions were understood in psychological terms as thoughts or judgments, in an approach known as "<a href="/wiki/Psychologism" title="Psychologism">psychologism</a>". This position was heavily criticized around the turn of the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kusch_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kusch-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rush_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rush-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Internal structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Premises and conclusions have internal structure. As propositions or sentences, they can be either simple or complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-King-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickel_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pickel-45">[44]</a></sup> A complex proposition has other propositions as its constituents, which are linked to each other through <a href="/wiki/Logical_connective" title="Logical connective">propositional connectives</a> like "and" or "if...then". Simple propositions, on the other hand, do not have propositional parts. But they can also be conceived as having an internal structure: they are made up of subpropositional parts, like <a href="/wiki/Singular_term" title="Singular term">singular terms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(grammar)" title="Predicate (grammar)">predicates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-King-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickel_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pickel-45">[44]</a></sup> For example, the simple proposition "Mars is red" can be formed by applying the predicate "red" to the singular term "Mars".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-38">[38]</a></sup> In contrast, the complex proposition "Mars is red and Venus is white" is made up of two simple propositions connected by the propositional connective "and".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whether a proposition is true depends, at least in part, on its constituents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickel_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pickel-45">[44]</a></sup> For complex propositions formed using <a href="/wiki/Truth_function" title="Truth function">truth-functional</a> propositional connectives, their truth only depends on the truth values of their parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_38-5"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-38">[38]</a></sup> But this relation is more complicated in the case of simple propositions and their subpropositional parts. These subpropositional parts have meanings of their own, like referring to objects or classes of objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_38-6"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Routledge_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michaelson_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michaelson-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickel_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pickel-45">[44]</a></sup> Whether the simple proposition they form is true depends on their relation to reality, i.e. what the objects they refer to are like. This topic is studied by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theories_of_reference" title="Theories of reference">theories of reference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michaelson_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Michaelson-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Logical_truth">Logical truth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Logical truth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Logical_truth" title="Logical truth">logical truth</a></div>
<p>In some cases, a simple or a complex proposition is true independently of the substantive meanings of its parts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacFarlane_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacFarlane-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup> For example, the complex proposition "if Mars is red, then Mars is red" is true independent of whether its parts, i.e. the simple proposition "Mars is red", are true or false. In such cases, the truth is called a <a href="/wiki/Logical_truth" title="Logical truth">logical truth</a>: a proposition is logically true if its truth depends only on the logical vocabulary used in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacFarlane_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacFarlane-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GÃ³mez-Torrente_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-GÃ³mez-Torrente-17">[17]</a></sup> This means that it is true under all <a href="/wiki/Interpretation_(logic)" title="Interpretation (logic)">interpretations</a> of its non-logical terms. In some <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logics</a>, this notion can be understood equivalently as truth at all <a href="/wiki/Possible_world" title="Possible world">possible worlds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GÃ³mez-Torrente_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-GÃ³mez-Torrente-17">[17]</a></sup> Logical truth plays an important role in logic and some theorists even define logic as the study of logical truths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Truth_tables">Truth tables</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Truth tables">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Truth_table" title="Truth table">Truth tables</a> can be used to show how logical connectives work or how the truth of complex propositions depends on their parts. They have a column for each input variable. Each row corresponds to one possible combination of the <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">truth values</a> these variables can take. The final columns present the truth values of the corresponding expressions as determined by the input values. For example, the expression <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p\land q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

It could be used to express a sentence like "yesterday was Sunday and the weather was good". It is only true if both of its input variables, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

In all other cases, the expression as a whole is false. Other important logical connectives are <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \lor }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

For example, given the conditional proposition <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p\to q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
