<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arguments_and_inferences">Arguments and inferences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Arguments and inferences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">argument</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inference</a></div>
<p>Logic is commonly defined in terms of <a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">arguments</a> or <a href="/wiki/Inference" title="Inference">inferences</a> as the study of their correctness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-7"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup> An <i>argument</i> is a set of premises together with a conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> An <i>inference</i> is the process of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reasoning" title="Reasoning">reasoning</a> from these premises to the conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-8"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup> But these terms are often used interchangeably in logic. Arguments are correct or incorrect depending on whether their premises support their conclusion. Premises and conclusions, on the other hand, are true or false depending on whether they are in accord with <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a>. In formal logic, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soundness_(logic)" title="Soundness (logic)">sound</a> argument is an argument that is both correct and has only true premises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Sometimes a distinction is made between simple and complex arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-9"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup> A complex argument is made up of a chain of simple arguments. These simple arguments constitute a <i>chain</i> because the conclusions of the earlier arguments are used as premises in the later arguments. For a complex argument to be successful, each link of the chain has to be successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-10"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Argument_terminology_used_in_logic_(en).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="744" data-file-width="1052" decoding="async" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Argument_terminology_used_in_logic_%28en%29.svg/400px-Argument_terminology_used_in_logic_%28en%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Argument_terminology_used_in_logic_%28en%29.svg/600px-Argument_terminology_used_in_logic_%28en%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Argument_terminology_used_in_logic_%28en%29.svg/800px-Argument_terminology_used_in_logic_%28en%29.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Argument_terminology_used_in_logic_(en).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Argument" title="Argument">Argument</a> terminology used in logic</div></div></div>
<p>Arguments and inferences are either are correct or incorrect. If they are correct then their premises support their conclusion. In the incorrect case, this support is missing. It can take different forms corresponding to the different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Method_of_reasoning" title="Method of reasoning">types of reasoning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backmann_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Backmann-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPDeductiveInductive_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPDeductiveInductive-54">[53]</a></sup> The strongest form of support corresponds to <a href="/wiki/Deductive_reasoning" title="Deductive reasoning">deductive reasoning</a>. But even arguments that are not deductively valid may still constitute good arguments because their premises offer non-deductive support to their conclusions. For such cases, the term <i><a href="/wiki/Ampliative" title="Ampliative">ampliative</a></i> or <i>inductive reasoning</i> is used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backmann_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Backmann-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPDeductiveInductive_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEPDeductiveInductive-54">[53]</a></sup> Deductive arguments are associated with formal logic in contrast to the relation between ampliative arguments and informal logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weddle_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weddle-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInformal_19-6"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInformal-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deductive">Deductive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Deductive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A <i>deductively <a href="/wiki/Validity_(logic)" title="Validity (logic)">valid</a> argument</i> is one whose premises guarantee the truth of its conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RoutledgeFormalInformal_16-9"><a href="#cite_note-RoutledgeFormalInformal-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKeon_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-McKeon-8">[8]</a></sup> For instance, the argument "Victoria is tall; Victoria has brown hair; therefore Victoria is tall and has brown hair" is deductively valid. For deductive validity, it does not matter whether the premises or the conclusion are actually true. So the argument "trees can speak the English language; therefore trees can speak a language" is valid because, if the premise were true, the conclusion would be true as well.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Alfred_Tarski" title="Alfred Tarski">Alfred Tarski</a> holds that deductive arguments have three essential features: (1) they are formal, i.e. they depend only on the form of the premises and the conclusion; (2) they are a priori, i.e. no sense experience is needed to determine whether they obtain; (3) they are modal, i.e. that they hold by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logical_necessity" title="Logical necessity">logical necessity</a> for the given propositions, independent of any other circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKeon_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-McKeon-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the first feature, the focus on formality, deductive inference is usually identified with <a href="/wiki/Rule_of_inference" title="Rule of inference">rules of inference</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-12"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup> Rules of inference specify how the premises and the conclusion have to be structured for the inference to be valid. Arguments that do not follow any rule of inference are deductively invalid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-13"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackburn_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blackburn-55">[54]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Modus_ponens" title="Modus ponens">modus ponens</a> is a prominent rule of inference. It has the form "<i>p</i>; if <i>p</i>, then <i>q</i>; therefore <i>q</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackburn_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blackburn-55">[54]</a></sup> Knowing that it has just rained (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>p</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle p}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="p" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81eac1e205430d1f40810df36a0edffdc367af36" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:1.259ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>) and that after rain the streets are wet (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle p\to q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>p</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">â<!-- â --></mo>
<mi>q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle p\to q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="p\to q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8fccb3827df1efe7930f9d1febd15d2359971b93" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:5.942ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>), one can use modus ponens to deduce that the streets are wet (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/06809d64fa7c817ffc7e323f85997f783dbdf71d" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.07ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>).
</p><p>The third feature can be expressed by stating that deductively valid inferences are truth-preserving: it is impossible for the premises to be true and the conclusion to be false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-14"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backmann_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Backmann-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPDeductiveInductive_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEPDeductiveInductive-54">[53]</a></sup> Because of this feature, it is often asserted that deductive inferences are uninformative since the conclusion cannot arrive at new <a href="/wiki/Information" title="Information">information</a> not already present in the premises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-15"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D'Agostino_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-D'Agostino-29">[29]</a></sup> But this point is not always accepted since it would mean, for example, that most of <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">mathematics</a> is uninformative. A different characterization distinguishes between surface and depth information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-16"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SagÃ¼illo_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-SagÃ¼illo-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka2_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka2-58">[57]</a></sup> On this view, deductive inferences are uninformative on the depth level but can be highly informative on the surface level, as may be the case for various mathematical proofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-17"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SagÃ¼illo_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-SagÃ¼illo-57">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka2_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka2-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ampliative">Ampliative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ampliative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Ampliative inferences, on the other hand, are informative even on the depth level. They are more interesting in this sense since the thinker may acquire substantive information from them and thereby learn something genuinely new.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-18"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backmann_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-Backmann-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPArguments_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEPArguments-31">[31]</a></sup> But this feature comes with a certain cost: the premises support the conclusion in the sense that they make its truth more likely but they do not ensure its truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-19"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backmann_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-Backmann-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPArguments_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEPArguments-31">[31]</a></sup> This means that the conclusion of an ampliative argument may be false even though all its premises are true. This characteristic is closely related to <i><a href="/wiki/Non-monotonic_logic" title="Non-monotonic logic">non-monotonicity</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Defeasible_reasoning" title="Defeasible reasoning">defeasibility</a></i>: it may be necessary to retract an earlier conclusion upon receiving new information or in the light of new inferences drawn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-20"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Backmann_30-6"><a href="#cite_note-Backmann-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPDeductiveInductive_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-IEPDeductiveInductive-54">[53]</a></sup> Ampliative reasoning is of central importance since many arguments found in everyday discourse and the <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">sciences</a> are ampliative. Ampliative arguments are not automatically incorrect. Instead, they just follow different standards of correctness. An important aspect of most ampliative arguments is that the support they provide for their conclusion comes in degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPDeductiveInductive_54-4"><a href="#cite_note-IEPDeductiveInductive-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInductive_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInductive-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup> In this sense, the line between correct and incorrect arguments is blurry in some cases, as when the premises offer weak but non-negligible support. This contrasts with deductive arguments, which are either valid or invalid with nothing in-between.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPDeductiveInductive_54-5"><a href="#cite_note-IEPDeductiveInductive-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInductive_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInductive-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-3"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The terminology used to categorize ampliative arguments is inconsistent. Some authors use the term "induction" to cover all forms of non-deductive arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPDeductiveInductive_54-6"><a href="#cite_note-IEPDeductiveInductive-54">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInductive_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInductive-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacmillanInduction_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacmillanInduction-61">[60]</a></sup> But in a more narrow sense, <i><a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">induction</a></i> is only one type of ampliative argument besides <i><a href="/wiki/Abductive_reasoning" title="Abductive reasoning">abductive</a> arguments</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-4"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup> Some authors also allow <i>conductive arguments</i> as one more type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInformal_19-7"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInformal-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> In this narrow sense, induction is often defined as a form of <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistical</a> generalization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> In this case, the premises of an inductive argument are many individual observations that all show a certain pattern. The conclusion then is a general law that this pattern always obtains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacmillanInduction_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacmillanInduction-61">[60]</a></sup> In this sense, one may infer that "all elephants are gray" based on one's past observations of the color of elephants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-5"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup> A closely related form of inductive inference has as its conclusion not a general law but one more specific instance, as when it is inferred that an elephant one has not seen yet is also gray.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacmillanInduction_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-MacmillanInduction-61">[60]</a></sup> Some theorists stipulate that inductive inferences rest only on statistical considerations in order to distinguish them from abductive inference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-6"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Abductive inference may or may not take statistical observations into consideration. In either case, the premises offer support for the conclusion because the conclusion is the best <a href="/wiki/Explanation" title="Explanation">explanation</a> of why the premises are true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-7"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koslowski_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koslowski-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abduction_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-abduction-66">[ii]</a></sup> In this sense, abduction is also called the <i>inference to the best explanation</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cummings_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cummings-67">[65]</a></sup> For example, given the premise that there is a plate with breadcrumbs in the kitchen in the early morning, one may infer the conclusion that one's house-mate had a midnight snack and was too tired to clean the table. This conclusion is justified because it is the best explanation of the current state of the kitchen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-8"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup> For abduction, it is not sufficient that the conclusion explains the premises. For example, the conclusion that a burglar broke into the house last night, got hungry on the job, and had a midnight snack, would also explain the state of the kitchen. But this conclusion is not justified because it is not the best or most likely explanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAbduction_56-9"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAbduction-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koslowski_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koslowski-65">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cummings_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cummings-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fallacies">Fallacies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fallacies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Not all arguments live up to the standards of correct reasoning. When they do not, they are usually referred to as <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">fallacies</a>. Their central aspect is not that their conclusion is false but that there is some flaw with the reasoning leading to this conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaThought_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaThought-68">[66]</a></sup> So the argument "it is sunny today; therefore spiders have eight legs" is fallacious even though the conclusion is true. Some theorists give a more restrictive definition of fallacies by additionally requiring that they appear to be correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup> This way, genuine fallacies can be distinguished from mere mistakes of reasoning due to carelessness. This explains why people tend to commit fallacies: because they have an alluring element that seduces people into committing and accepting them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup> However, this reference to appearances is controversial because it belongs to the field of <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, not logic, and because appearances may be different for different people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton3_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walton3-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_America%27s_dilemma_-_Dalrymple._LCCN2010651418.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5396" data-file-width="8615" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Young_America%27s_dilemma_-_Dalrymple._LCCN2010651418.jpg/330px-Young_America%27s_dilemma_-_Dalrymple._LCCN2010651418.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Young_America%27s_dilemma_-_Dalrymple._LCCN2010651418.jpg/495px-Young_America%27s_dilemma_-_Dalrymple._LCCN2010651418.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Young_America%27s_dilemma_-_Dalrymple._LCCN2010651418.jpg/660px-Young_America%27s_dilemma_-_Dalrymple._LCCN2010651418.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_America%27s_dilemma_-_Dalrymple._LCCN2010651418.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young America's dilemma: Shall I be wise and great, or rich and powerful? (poster from 1901) This is an example of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/False_Dilemma" title="False Dilemma">false Dilemma</a>: an informal fallacy using a disjunctive premise that excludes viable alternatives.</div></div></div>
<p>Fallacies are usually divided into <a href="/wiki/Formal_fallacy" title="Formal fallacy">formal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Informal_fallacies" title="Informal fallacies">informal fallacies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VleetIntro_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-VleetIntro-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dowden_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-Dowden-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stump_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stump-35">[35]</a></sup> For formal fallacies, the source of the error is found in the <i>form</i> of the argument. For example, <a href="/wiki/Denying_the_antecedent" title="Denying the antecedent">denying the antecedent</a> is one type of formal fallacy, as in "if Othello is a bachelor, then he is male; Othello is not a bachelor; therefore Othello is not male".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaThought_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaThought-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> But most fallacies fall into the category of informal fallacies, of which a great variety is discussed in the academic literature. The source of their error is usually found in the <i>content</i> or the <i>context</i> of the argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dowden_34-4"><a href="#cite_note-Dowden-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup> Informal fallacies are sometimes categorized as fallacies of ambiguity, fallacies of presumption, or fallacies of relevance. For fallacies of ambiguity, the ambiguity and vagueness of <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">natural language</a> are responsible for their flaw, as in "feathers are light; what is light cannot be dark; therefore feathers cannot be dark".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engel2_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Engel2-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackie_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stump_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stump-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup> Fallacies of presumption have a wrong or unjustified premise but may be valid otherwise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engel4_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engel4-71">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stump_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stump-35">[35]</a></sup> In the case of fallacies of relevance, the premises do not support the conclusion because they are not relevant to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackie_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stump_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-Stump-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitory_and_strategic_rules">Definitory and strategic rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Definitory and strategic rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The main focus of most logicians is to investigate the criteria according to which an argument is correct or incorrect. A fallacy is committed if these criteria are violated. In the case of formal logic, they are known as <i>rules of inference</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-21"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup> They constitute definitory rules, which determine whether a certain inference is correct or which inferences are allowed. Definitory rules contrast with strategic rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-22"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pedemonte_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pedemonte-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaSystems_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaSystems-73">[71]</a></sup> Strategic rules specify which inferential moves are necessary in order to reach a given conclusion based on a certain set of premises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-23"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pedemonte_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pedemonte-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaSystems_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaSystems-73">[71]</a></sup> This distinction does not just apply to logic but also to various games as well. In <a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">chess</a>, for example, the definitory rules dictate that <a href="/wiki/Bishop_(chess)" title="Bishop (chess)">bishops</a> may only move diagonally while the strategic rules describe how the allowed moves may be used to win a game, for example, by controlling the center and by defending one's <a href="/wiki/King_(chess)" title="King (chess)">king</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-24"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pedemonte_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pedemonte-72">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaSystems_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaSystems-73">[71]</a></sup> A third type of rules concerns <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">empirical</a> descriptive rules. They belong to the field of psychology and generalize how people actually draw inferences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-25"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup> It has been argued that logicians should give more emphasis to strategic rules since they are highly relevant for effective reasoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-26"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_systems">Formal systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Formal systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Formal_system" title="Formal system">Formal system</a></div>
<p>A formal system of logic consists of a <a href="/wiki/Formal_language" title="Formal language">language</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proof_system" title="Proof system">proof system</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Semantics_of_logic" title="Semantics of logic">semantics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classicallogicsep_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-classicallogicsep-74">[72]</a></sup> The term "a logic" is often used a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Countable_noun" title="Countable noun">countable noun</a> to refer to a particular formal system of logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacquette_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquette-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-27"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup> Starting in the 20th century, <a href="#Systems_of_logic">many new formal systems</a> have been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[iii]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacquette_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquette-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-28"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_language">Formal language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Formal language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Formal_language" title="Formal language">Formal language</a></div>
<p>A <i>formal language</i> consists of an <i>alphabet</i> and syntactic rules. The alphabet is the set of basic symbols used in <a href="/wiki/Expression_(mathematics)" title="Expression (mathematics)">expressions</a>. The syntactic rules determine how these symbols may be arranged to result in <a href="/wiki/Well-formed_formula" title="Well-formed formula">well-formed formulas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classicallogicsep_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-classicallogicsep-74">[72]</a></sup> For instance, the syntactic rules of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propositional_logic" title="Propositional logic">propositional logic</a> determine that <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P\land Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P\land Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P\land Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c5690bb4822d8c821a00cfe3c6644b046a884af4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.166ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>"</span> is a well-formed formula but <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle PQ\land \land \land }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mi>P</mi>
<mi>Q</mi>
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle PQ\land \land \land }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle PQ\land \land \land }" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d920de07195586017948c53c5007ae7efd5bdce6" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:9.267ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>"</span> is not.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Proof_system">Proof system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Proof system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proof_system" title="Proof system">Proof system</a></div>
<p>A <i>proof system</i> is a collection of formal rules which define when a conclusion follows from given premises. For instance, the classical rule of <a href="/wiki/Conjunction_introduction" title="Conjunction introduction">conjunction introduction</a> states that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P\land Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P\land Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P\land Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c5690bb4822d8c821a00cfe3c6644b046a884af4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.166ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> follows from the premises <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8752c7023b4b3286800fe3238271bbca681219ed" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.838ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>. Rules in a proof systems are always defined in terms of formulas' syntactic form, never in terms of their meanings. Such rules can be applied sequentially, giving a mechanical procedure for generating conclusions from premises. There are a number of different types of proof systems including <a href="/wiki/Natural_deduction" title="Natural deduction">natural deduction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sequent_calculus" title="Sequent calculus">sequent calculi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classicallogicsep_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-classicallogicsep-74">[72]</a></sup> Proof systems are closely linked to philosophical work which characterizes logic as the study of valid inference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Semantics">Semantics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Semantics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Semantics_of_logic" title="Semantics of logic">Semantics of logic</a></div>
<p>A <i>semantics</i> is a system for <a href="/wiki/Map_(mathematics)" title="Map (mathematics)">mapping</a> expressions of a formal language to their denotations. In many systems of logic, denotations are <a href="/wiki/Truth_value" title="Truth value">truth values</a>. For instance, the semantics for <a href="/wiki/Classical_logic" title="Classical logic">classical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propositional_logic" title="Propositional logic">propositional logic</a> assigns the formula <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P\land Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P\land Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle P\land Q}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c5690bb4822d8c821a00cfe3c6644b046a884af4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.166ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> the denotation "true" whenever <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8752c7023b4b3286800fe3238271bbca681219ed" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.838ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> are true. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entailment" title="Entailment">Entailment</a> is a semantic relation which holds between formulas when the first cannot be true without the second being true as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classicallogicsep1_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-classicallogicsep1-82">[79]</a></sup> Semantics is closely tied to the philosophical characterization of logic as the study of <a href="/wiki/Logical_truth" title="Logical truth">logical truth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-29"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Soundness_and_completeness">Soundness and completeness</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Soundness and completeness">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Soundness" title="Soundness">Soundness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Completeness_(logic)" title="Completeness (logic)">completeness (logic)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metalogic" title="Metalogic">metalogic</a></div>
<p>A system of logic is <i>sound</i> when its proof system cannot derive a conclusion from a set of premises unless it is semantically entailed by them. In other words, its proof system cannot lead to false conclusions, as defined by the semantics. A system is <i>complete</i> when its proof system can derive every conclusion that is semantically entailed by its premises. In other words, its proof system can lead to any true conclusion, as defined by the semantics. Thus, soundness and completeness together describe a system whose notions of validity and entailment line up perfectly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The study of properties of formal systems is called <i>metalogic</i>. Other important metalogical properties include <i><a href="/wiki/Consistency" title="Consistency">consistency</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Decidability_(logic)" title="Decidability (logic)">decidability</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Expressive_power_(computer_science)" title="Expressive power (computer science)">expressive power</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems_of_logic">Systems of logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Systems of logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Systems of logic are theoretical frameworks for assessing the correctness of reasoning and arguments. For over two thousand years, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotelian_logic" title="Aristotelian logic">Aristotelian logic</a> was treated as the canon of logic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacquette_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquette-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPAristotle_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-IEPAristotle-88">[85]</a></sup> but modern developments in this field have led to a vast proliferation of logical systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackDeviant1_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-HaackDeviant1-89">[86]</a></sup> One prominent categorization divides modern formal logical systems into <a href="/wiki/Classical_logic" title="Classical logic">classical logic</a>, extended logics, and <a href="/wiki/Deviant_logic" title="Deviant logic">deviant logics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackDeviant1_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-HaackDeviant1-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolf_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolf-90">[87]</a></sup> Classical logic is to be distinguished from traditional or Aristotelian logic. It encompasses propositional logic and first-order logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-30"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup> It is "classical" in the sense that it is based on various fundamental logical intuitions shared by most logicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-31"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup> These intuitions include the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_excluded_middle" title="Law of excluded middle">law of excluded middle</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double_negation_elimination" title="Double negation elimination">double negation elimination</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_explosion" title="Principle of explosion">principle of explosion</a>, and the bivalence of truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shapiro_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shapiro-91">[88]</a></sup> It was originally developed to analyze mathematical arguments and was only later applied to other fields as well. Because of this focus on mathematics, it does not include logical vocabulary relevant to many other topics of philosophical importance, like the distinction between necessity and possibility, the problem of ethical obligation and permission, or the relations between past, present, and future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgess1_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess1-92">[89]</a></sup> Such issues are addressed by extended logics. They build on the fundamental intuitions of classical logic and expand it by introducing new logical vocabulary. This way, the exact logical approach is applied to fields like <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> that lie beyond the scope of mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacMillanNonClassical_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacMillanNonClassical-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacquette_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquette-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goble_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goble-94">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Deviant logics, on the other hand, reject some of the fundamental intuitions of classical logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackDeviant1_89-2"><a href="#cite_note-HaackDeviant1-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolf_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wolf-90">[87]</a></sup> Because of this, they are usually seen not as its supplements but as its rivals. Deviant logical systems differ from each other either because they reject different classical intuitions or because they propose different alternatives to the same issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackDeviant1_89-3"><a href="#cite_note-HaackDeviant1-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolf_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wolf-90">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Informal logic is usually carried out in a less systematic way. It often focuses on more specific issues, like investigating a particular type of fallacy or studying a certain aspect of argumentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordInformal_19-8"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordInformal-19">[19]</a></sup> Nonetheless, some systems of informal logic have also been presented that try to provide a systematic characterization of the correctness of arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korb_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Korb-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritola_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ritola-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Aristotelian">Aristotelian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Aristotelian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Aristotelian logic encompasses a great variety of topics, including <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> theses about <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontological</a> categories and problems of scientific explanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPAristotle_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-IEPAristotle-88">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAncient_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAncient-96">[93]</a></sup> But in a more narrow sense, it refers to <a href="/wiki/Term_logic" title="Term logic">term logic</a> or syllogistics. A <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogism</a> is a certain form of argument involving three propositions: two premises and a conclusion. Each proposition has three essential parts: a <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">subject</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(grammar)" title="Predicate (grammar)">predicate</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Copula_(linguistics)" title="Copula (linguistics)">copula</a> connecting the subject to the predicate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPAristotle_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-IEPAristotle-88">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAncient_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAncient-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaAristotle_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaAristotle-97">[94]</a></sup> For example, the proposition "Socrates is wise" is made up of the subject "Socrates", the predicate "wise", and the copula "is".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPAristotle_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-IEPAristotle-88">[85]</a></sup> The subject and the predicate are the <i>terms</i> of the proposition. In this sense, Aristotelian logic does not contain complex propositions made up of various simple propositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup> It differs in this aspect from propositional logic, in which any two propositions can be linked using a logical connective like "and" to form a new complex proposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Square_of_opposition,_set_diagrams.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="620" data-file-width="479" decoding="async" height="388" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Square_of_opposition%2C_set_diagrams.svg/300px-Square_of_opposition%2C_set_diagrams.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Square_of_opposition%2C_set_diagrams.svg/450px-Square_of_opposition%2C_set_diagrams.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Square_of_opposition%2C_set_diagrams.svg/600px-Square_of_opposition%2C_set_diagrams.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Square_of_opposition,_set_diagrams.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Square_of_opposition" title="Square of opposition">square of opposition</a> is often used to visualize the relations between the four basic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Categorical_propositions" title="Categorical propositions">categorical propositions</a> in Aristotelian logic. It shows, for example, that the propositions "All S are P" and "Some S are not P" are contradictory, meaning that one of them has to be true while the other is false.</div></div></div>
<p>Aristotelian logic differs from predicate logic in that the subject is either <i>universal</i>, <i>particular</i>, <i>indefinite</i>, or <i>singular</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAncient_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAncient-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaAristotle_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaAristotle-97">[94]</a></sup> For example, the term "all humans" is a universal subject in the proposition "all humans are mortal". A similar proposition could be formed by replacing it with the particular term "some humans", the indefinite term "a human", or the singular term "Socrates".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAncient_96-3"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAncient-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritannicaAristotle_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-BritannicaAristotle-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-5"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup> In predicate logic, on the other hand, universal and particular propositions would be expressed by using a <a href="/wiki/Quantifier_(logic)" title="Quantifier (logic)">quantifier</a> and two predicates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-6"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup> Another important difference is that Aristotelian logic only includes predicates for simple <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">properties</a> of entities, but lacks predicates corresponding to <a href="/wiki/Relations_(philosophy)" title="Relations (philosophy)">relations</a> between entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup> The predicate can be linked to the subject in two ways: either by affirming it or by denying it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-7"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPAristotle_88-4"><a href="#cite_note-IEPAristotle-88">[85]</a></sup> For example, the proposition "Socrates is not a cat" involves the denial of the predicate "cat" to the subject "Socrates". Using different combinations of subjects and predicates, a great variety of propositions and syllogisms can be formed. Syllogisms are characterized by the fact that the premises are linked to each other and to the conclusion by sharing one predicate in each case.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-8"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurley_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hurley-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copi_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copi-101">[98]</a></sup> Thus, these three propositions contain three predicates, referred to as <i>major term</i>, <i>minor term</i>, and <i>middle term</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurley_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hurley-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copi_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Copi-101">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPAristotle_88-5"><a href="#cite_note-IEPAristotle-88">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAncient_96-4"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAncient-96">[93]</a></sup> The central aspect of Aristotelian logic involves classifying all possible syllogisms into valid and invalid arguments according to how the propositions are formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordAristotle_87-9"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordAristotle-87">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPAristotle_88-6"><a href="#cite_note-IEPAristotle-88">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hurley_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hurley-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copi_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-Copi-101">[98]</a></sup> For example, the syllogism "all men are mortal; Socrates is a man; therefore Socrates is mortal" is valid. The syllogism "all cats are mortal; Socrates is mortal; therefore Socrates is a cat", on the other hand, is invalid.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical">Classical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Classical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Propositional_logic">Propositional logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Propositional logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Propositional_calculus" title="Propositional calculus">Propositional calculus</a></div>
<p>Propositional logic comprises formal systems in which formulae are built from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_propositions" title="Atomic propositions">atomic propositions</a> using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logical_connectives" title="Logical connectives">logical connectives</a>. For instance, propositional logic represents the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjunction_(logic)" title="Conjunction (logic)">conjunction</a> of two atomic propositions <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8752c7023b4b3286800fe3238271bbca681219ed" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.838ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span> as the complex formula <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P\land Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P\land Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P\land Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c5690bb4822d8c821a00cfe3c6644b046a884af4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:6.166ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>. Unlike predicate logic where terms and predicates are the smallest units, propositional logic takes full propositions with truth values as its most basic component.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brody_2006_535â536_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brody_2006_535â536-102">[99]</a></sup> Thus, propositional logics can only represent logical relationships that arise from the way complex propositions are built from simpler ones; it cannot represent inferences that results from the inner structure of a proposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="First-order_logic">First-order logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: First-order logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BS-12-Begriffsschrift_Quantifier1-svg.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="154" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="66" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/BS-12-Begriffsschrift_Quantifier1-svg.svg/220px-BS-12-Begriffsschrift_Quantifier1-svg.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/BS-12-Begriffsschrift_Quantifier1-svg.svg/330px-BS-12-Begriffsschrift_Quantifier1-svg.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/BS-12-Begriffsschrift_Quantifier1-svg.svg/440px-BS-12-Begriffsschrift_Quantifier1-svg.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BS-12-Begriffsschrift_Quantifier1-svg.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Begriffschrift" title="Begriffschrift">Begriffschrift</a></i> introduced the notion of quantifier in a graphical notation, which here represents the judgement that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \forall x.F(x)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">â<!-- â --></mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo>.</mo>
<mi>F</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \forall x.F(x)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \forall x.F(x)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ba23799cf40a6e9b0a427c429dc89fbb8a74bfee" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.536ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> is true.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/First-order_logic" title="First-order logic">First-order logic</a></div>
<p>First-order logic includes the same propositional connectives as propositional logic but differs from it because it articulates the internal structure of propositions. This happens through devices such as <a href="/wiki/Singular_term" title="Singular term">singular terms</a>, which refer to particular objects, <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(mathematical_logic)" title="Predicate (mathematical logic)">predicates</a>, which refer to properties and relations, and <a href="/wiki/Quantifier_(logic)" title="Quantifier (logic)">quantifiers</a>, which treat notions like "some" and "all".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxford_38-7"><a href="#cite_note-Oxford-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-King-44">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michaelson_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Michaelson-47">[46]</a></sup> For example, to express the proposition "this raven is black", one may use the predicate <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle B}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>B</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle B}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="B" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/47136aad860d145f75f3eed3022df827cee94d7a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.764ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> for the property "black" and the singular term <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle r}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>r</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle r}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="r" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0d1ecb613aa2984f0576f70f86650b7c2a132538" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.049ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> referring to the raven to form the expression <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle B(r)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>B</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>r</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle B(r)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle B(r)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fdf985b9cc5e851d080440261106d69b6d61dd88" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:4.622ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. To express that some objects are black, the existential quantifier <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exists }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\exists " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/77ed842b6b90b2fdd825320cf8e5265fa937b583" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.293ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is combined with the variable <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>x</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle x}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span> to form the proposition <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exists xB(x)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">â<!-- â --></mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mi>B</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \exists xB(x)}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \exists xB(x)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f4426e051732eddd927ca9b665c90a8b7e0b8e0" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.525ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>. First-order logic contains various rules of inference that determine how expressions articulated this way can form valid arguments, for example, that one may infer <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exists xB(x)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \exists xB(x)}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f4426e051732eddd927ca9b665c90a8b7e0b8e0" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:7.525ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span> from <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle B(r)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Young_frege.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="619" data-file-width="458" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Young_frege.jpg/220px-Young_frege.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Young_frege.jpg/330px-Young_frege.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Young_frege.jpg/440px-Young_frege.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Young_frege.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gottlob Frege developed the first fully axiomatic system of first-order logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The development of first-order logic is usually attributed to <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>, who is also credited as one of the founders of <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic philosophy</a>. However, the formulation of first-order logic most often used today is found in <a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Mathematical_Logic" title="Principles of Mathematical Logic">Principles of Mathematical Logic</a> by <a href="/wiki/David_Hilbert" title="David Hilbert">David Hilbert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Ackermann" title="Wilhelm Ackermann">Wilhelm Ackermann</a> in 1928.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup> The analytical generality of first-order logic allowed the formalization of mathematics, drove the investigation of <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a>, and allowed the development of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Tarski" title="Alfred Tarski">Alfred Tarski</a>'s approach to <a href="/wiki/Model_theory" title="Model theory">model theory</a>. It provides the foundation of modern <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Extended">Extended</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Extended">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modal_logic">Modal logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Modal logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">Modal logic</a></div>
<p>Many extended logics take the form of modal logic by introducing modal operators. Modal logics were originally developed to represent statements about necessity and possibility. For instance the modal formula <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Diamond P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>â<!-- â --></mi>
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Diamond P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Diamond P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c11ced51ad343c329a468b33e5139a99306fd0b6" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:3.296ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> can be read as "possibly <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>" while <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Box P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>â»<!-- â» --></mi>
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Box P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Box P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d30b6d37f2d650ad3011989f5477df0536bed7d1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.554ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> can be read as "necessarily <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>". Modal logics can be used to represent different phenomena depending on what <i>flavor</i> of necessity and possibility is under consideration. When <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Box }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>â»<!-- â» --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Box }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Box " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/029b77f09ebeaf7528fc831fe57848be51f2240b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.808ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is used to represent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistemic_logic" title="Epistemic logic">epistemic necessity</a>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Box P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
