<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is known. When <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Box }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>â»<!-- â» --></mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Box }</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Box " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/029b77f09ebeaf7528fc831fe57848be51f2240b" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.808ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is used to represent <a href="/wiki/Deontic_logic" title="Deontic logic">deontic necessity</a>, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \Box P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>â»<!-- â» --></mi>
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \Box P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\Box P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d30b6d37f2d650ad3011989f5477df0536bed7d1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.554ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> states that <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle P}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>P</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle P}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="P" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b4dc73bf40314945ff376bd363916a738548d40a" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.745ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is a moral or legal obligation. Within philosophy, modal logics are widely used in <a href="/wiki/Formal_epistemology" title="Formal epistemology">formal epistemology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Formal_ethics" title="Formal ethics">formal ethics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>. Within <a href="/wiki/Formal_semantics_(natural_language)" title="Formal semantics (natural language)">linguistic semantics</a>, systems based on modal logic are used to analyze <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguistic_modality" title="Linguistic modality">linguistic modality</a> in natural languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bluebible_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-bluebible-111">[108]</a></sup> Other fields such as <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a> have applied the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relational_semantics" title="Relational semantics">relational semantics</a> for modal logic beyond its original conceptual motivation, using it to provide insight into patterns including the <a href="/wiki/Multiverse_(set_theory)" title="Multiverse (set theory)">set-theoretic multiverse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transition_system" title="Transition system">transition systems</a> in computation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bluebible_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-bluebible-111">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-openminds_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-openminds-112">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_order_logic">Higher order logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Higher order logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Higher-order_logic" title="Higher-order logic">Higher-order logic</a></div>
<p>Higher-order logics extend classical logic not by using modal operators but by introducing new forms of <a href="/wiki/Quantifier_(logic)" title="Quantifier (logic)">quantification</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-11"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VÃ¤Ã¤nÃ¤nen_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-VÃ¤Ã¤nÃ¤nen-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ketland_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ketland-114">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Computing_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Computing-115">[112]</a></sup> Quantifiers correspond to terms like "all" or "some". In classical first-order logic, quantifiers are only applied to individuals. The formula <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exists x(Apple(x)\land Sweet(x))}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi mathvariant="normal">â<!-- â --></mi>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>A</mi>
<mi>p</mi>
<mi>p</mi>
<mi>l</mi>
<mi>e</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>
<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo>â§<!-- â§ --></mo>
<mi>S</mi>
<mi>w</mi>
<mi>e</mi>

<mi>x</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \exists x(Apple(x)\land Sweet(x))}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \exists x(Apple(x)\land Sweet(x))}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/91553ca9cfa2cb1d9476194df2acbd86689b0d74" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:25.321ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>"</span> (<i>some</i> apples are sweet) is an example of the <a href="/wiki/Existential_quantification" title="Existential quantification">existential quantifier</a> <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exists }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\exists " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/77ed842b6b90b2fdd825320cf8e5265fa937b583" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.293ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>"</span> applied to the individual variable <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle x}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="x" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87f9e315fd7e2ba406057a97300593c4802b53e4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.33ex; height:1.676ex;"/></span>"</span>. In higher-order logics, quantification is also allowed over predicates. This increases its expressive power. For example, to express the idea that Mary and John share some qualities, one could use the formula <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \exists Q(Q(mary)\land Q(john))}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>
</mstyle>
</mrow>

</semantics>

In this case, the existential quantifier is applied to the predicate variable <span class="nowrap">"<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle Q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>Q</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Q}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="Q" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8752c7023b4b3286800fe3238271bbca681219ed" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.838ex; height:2.509ex;"/></span>"</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-12"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VÃ¤Ã¤nÃ¤nen_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-VÃ¤Ã¤nÃ¤nen-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ketland_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ketland-114">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Computing_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Computing-115">[112]</a></sup> The added expressive power is especially useful for mathematics since it allows for more succinct formulations of mathematical theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-13"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup> But it has various drawbacks in regard to its meta-logical properties and ontological implications, which is why first-order logic is still much more widely used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-14"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ketland_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ketland-114">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deviant">Deviant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Deviant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A great variety of deviant logics have been proposed. One major paradigm is <a href="/wiki/Intuitionistic_logic" title="Intuitionistic logic">intuitionistic logic</a>, which rejects the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_the_excluded_middle" title="Law of the excluded middle">law of the excluded middle</a>. Intuitionism was developed by the Dutch mathematicians <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L.E.J._Brouwer" title="L.E.J. Brouwer">L.E.J. Brouwer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arend_Heyting" title="Arend Heyting">Arend Heyting</a> to underpin their <a href="/wiki/Constructivism_(philosophy_of_mathematics)" title="Constructivism (philosophy of mathematics)">constructive approach to mathematics</a>, in which the existence of a mathematical object can only be proven by constructing it. Intuitionism was further pursued by <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Gentzen" title="Gerhard Gentzen">Gerhard Gentzen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kurt_G%C3%B6del" title="Kurt GÃ¶del">Kurt GÃ¶del</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Dummett" title="Michael Dummett">Michael Dummett</a>, among others. Intuitionistic logic is of great interest to computer scientists, as it is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constructive_logic" title="Constructive logic">constructive logic</a> and sees many applications, such as extracting verified programs from proofs and influencing the design of <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming languages</a> through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Formulae-as-types_correspondence" title="Formulae-as-types correspondence">formulae-as-types correspondence</a>. It is closely related to nonclassical systems such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%E2%80%93Dummett_logic" title="GÃ¶delâDummett logic">GÃ¶delâDummett logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inquisitive_semantics" title="Inquisitive semantics">inquisitive logic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-valued_logics" title="Multi-valued logics">Multi-valued logics</a> depart from classicality by rejecting the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_bivalence" title="Principle of bivalence">principle of bivalence</a> which requires all propositions to be either true or false. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Jan_%C5%81ukasiewicz" title="Jan Åukasiewicz">Jan Åukasiewicz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Cole_Kleene" title="Stephen Cole Kleene">Stephen Cole Kleene</a> both proposed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ternary_logic" title="Ternary logic">ternary logics</a> which have a third truth value representing that a statement's truth value is indeterminate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup> These logics have seen applications including to <a href="/wiki/Presupposition" title="Presupposition">presupposition</a> in linguistics. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuzzy_logics" title="Fuzzy logics">Fuzzy logics</a> are multivalued logics that have an infinite number of "degrees of truth", represented by a <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real number</a> between 0 and 1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanford_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-123">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Paraconsistent_logic" title="Paraconsistent logic">Paraconsistent logics</a> are logical systems that can deal with contradictions. They are formulated to avoid the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_explosion" title="Principle of explosion">principle of explosion</a>: for them, it is not the case that anything follows from a contradiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordParaconsistent_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordParaconsistent-124">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacMillanNonClassical_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacMillanNonClassical-93">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zach_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zach-125">[122]</a></sup> They are often motivated by <a href="/wiki/Dialetheism" title="Dialetheism">dialetheism</a>, the view that contradictions are real or that reality itself is contradictory. <a href="/wiki/Graham_Priest" title="Graham Priest">Graham Priest</a> is an important contemporary proponent of this position and similar views have been ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zach_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zach-125">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordParaconsistent_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordParaconsistent-124">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordDialetheism_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordDialetheism-127">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Informal">Informal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Informal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <i>pragmatic</i> or <i>dialogical approach</i> to informal logic sees arguments as <a href="/wiki/Speech_act" title="Speech act">speech acts</a> and not merely as a set of premises together with a conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korb_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Korb-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritola_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ritola-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup> As speech acts, they occur in a certain context, like a <a href="/wiki/Dialogue" title="Dialogue">dialogue</a>, which affects the standards of right and wrong arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-7"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritola_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ritola-95">[92]</a></sup> A prominent version by <a href="/wiki/Douglas_N._Walton" title="Douglas N. Walton">Douglas N. Walton</a> understands a dialogue as a game between two players.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup> The initial position of each player is characterized by the propositions to which they are committed and the conclusion they intend to prove. Dialogues are games of persuasion: each player has the goal of convincing the opponent of their own conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-8"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup> This is achieved by making arguments: arguments are the moves of the game.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-9"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritola_95-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ritola-95">[92]</a></sup> They affect to which propositions the players are committed. A winning move is a successful argument that takes the opponent's commitments as premises and shows how one's own conclusion follows from them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-10"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup> This is usually not possible straight away. For this reason, it is normally necessary to formulate a sequence of arguments as intermediary steps, each of which brings the opponent a little closer to one's intended conclusion. Besides these positive arguments leading one closer to victory, there are also negative arguments preventing the opponent's victory by denying their conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton_25-11"><a href="#cite_note-Walton-25">[25]</a></sup> Whether an argument is correct depends on whether it promotes the progress of the dialogue. <a href="/wiki/Fallacy" title="Fallacy">Fallacies</a>, on the other hand, are violations of the standards of proper argumentative rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walton3_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walton3-69">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup> These standards also depend on the type of dialogue. For example, the standards governing the scientific discourse differ from the standards in business negiotiations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritola_95-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ritola-95">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>epistemic approach</i> to informal logic, on the other hand, focuses on the <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemic</a> role of arguments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korb_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Korb-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-9"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup> It is based on the idea that arguments aim to increase our knowledge. They achieve this by linking justified beliefs to beliefs that are not yet justified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siegel_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Siegel-128">[125]</a></sup> Correct arguments succeed at expanding knowledge while fallacies are epistemic failures: they do not justify the belief in their conclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korb_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-Korb-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-10"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup> In this sense, logical normativity consists in epistemic success or <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siegel_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Siegel-128">[125]</a></sup> For example, the fallacy of begging the question is a <i>fallacy</i> because it fails to provide independent justification for its conclusion, even though it is deductively valid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Siegel_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-Siegel-128">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackie_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mackie-37">[37]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_epistemology" title="Bayesian epistemology">Bayesian approach</a> is one example of an epistemic approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-11"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup> Central to Bayesianism is not just whether the agent believes something but the degree to which they believe it, the so-called <i>credence</i>. Degrees of belief are understood as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subjective_probability" title="Subjective probability">subjective probabilities</a> in the believed proposition, i.e. as how certain the agent is that the proposition is true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Olsson_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Olsson-129">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HÃ¡jek_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-HÃ¡jek-130">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartmann_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartmann-131">[128]</a></sup> On this view, reasoning can be interpreted as a process of changing one's credences, often in reaction to new incoming information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-StanfordFallacies_32-12"><a href="#cite_note-StanfordFallacies-32">[32]</a></sup> Correct reasoning, and the arguments it is based on, follows the laws of probability, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_epistemology#Principle_of_conditionalization" title="Bayesian epistemology">principle of conditionalization</a>. Bad or irrational reasoning, on the other hand, violates these laws.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HÃ¡jek_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-HÃ¡jek-130">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Talbott_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Talbott-132">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korb_36-5"><a href="#cite_note-Korb-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Areas_of_research">Areas of research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Areas of research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Logic is studied in various fields. In many cases, this is done by applying its formal method to specific topics outside its scope, like to <a href="/wiki/Formal_ethics" title="Formal ethics">ethics</a> or <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup> In other cases, logic itself is made the subject of research in another discipline. This can happen in diverse ways, like by investigating the philosophical presuppositions of fundamental logical concepts, by interpreting and analyzing logic through mathematical structures, or by studying and comparing abstract properties of formal logical systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HaackLogics1_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-HaackLogics1-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_of_logic_and_philosophical_logic">Philosophy of logic and philosophical logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Philosophy of logic and philosophical logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_logic" title="Philosophy of logic">Philosophy of logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_logic" title="Philosophical logic">Philosophical logic</a></div>
<p><i>Philosophy of logic</i> is the philosophical discipline studying the scope and nature of logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cambridge_7-15"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup> It investigates many presuppositions implicit in logic, like how to define its fundamental concepts or the metaphysical assumptions associated with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacquette_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquette-18">[18]</a></sup> It is also concerned with how to classify the different logical systems and considers the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontological" title="Ontological">ontological</a> commitments they incur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-4">[4]</a></sup> <i>Philosophical logic</i> is one important area within the philosophy of logic. It studies the application of logical methods to philosophical problems in fields like <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>, ethics, and <a href="/wiki/Formal_epistemology" title="Formal epistemology">epistemology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burgess1_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burgess1-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacquette_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-Jacquette-18">[18]</a></sup> This application usually happens in the form of <a href="#Extended">extended</a> or <a href="#Deviant">deviant logical systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goble_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goble-94">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka_6-32"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematical_logic">Mathematical logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Mathematical logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">Mathematical logic</a></div>
<p>Mathematical logic is the study of logic within mathematics. Major subareas include <a href="/wiki/Model_theory" title="Model theory">model theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proof_theory" title="Proof theory">proof theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computability_theory" title="Computability theory">computability theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bertrand_Russell_1957.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1997" data-file-width="1583" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bertrand_Russell_1957.jpg/220px-Bertrand_Russell_1957.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bertrand_Russell_1957.jpg/330px-Bertrand_Russell_1957.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Bertrand_Russell_1957.jpg/440px-Bertrand_Russell_1957.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bertrand_Russell_1957.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bertrand Russell made various significant contributions to mathematical logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Research in mathematical logic commonly addresses the mathematical properties of formal systems of logic. However, it can also include attempts to use logic to analyze mathematical reasoning or to establish logic-based <a href="/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations of mathematics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Introduction_to_Elementary_Mathematical_Logic_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Introduction_to_Elementary_Mathematical_Logic-137">[134]</a></sup> The latter was a major concern in early 20th century mathematical logic, which pursued the program of <a href="/wiki/Logicism" title="Logicism">logicism</a> pioneered by philosopher-logicians such as <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>. Mathematical theories were supposed to be logical <a href="/wiki/Tautology_(logic)" title="Tautology (logic)">tautologies</a>, and the programme was to show this by means of a reduction of mathematics to logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Principia_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Principia-138">[135]</a></sup> The various attempts to carry this out met with failure, from the crippling of Frege's project in his <i>Grundgesetze</i> by <a href="/wiki/Russell%27s_paradox" title="Russell's paradox">Russell's paradox</a>, to the defeat of <a href="/wiki/Hilbert%27s_program" title="Hilbert's program">Hilbert's program</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorem" title="GÃ¶del's incompleteness theorem">GÃ¶del's incompleteness theorems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka2020b_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka2020b-139">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">Set theory</a> originated in the study of the infinite by <a href="/wiki/Georg_Cantor" title="Georg Cantor">Georg Cantor</a>, and it has been the source of many of the most challenging and important issues in mathematical logic. They include <a href="/wiki/Cantor%27s_theorem" title="Cantor's theorem">Cantor's theorem</a>, the status of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axiom_of_Choice" title="Axiom of Choice">Axiom of Choice</a>, the question of the independence of the <a href="/wiki/Continuum_hypothesis" title="Continuum hypothesis">continuum hypothesis</a>, and the modern debate on <a href="/wiki/Large_cardinal" title="Large cardinal">large cardinal</a> axioms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recursion_theory" title="Recursion theory">Recursion theory</a> captures the idea of computation in logical and <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a> terms; its most classical achievements are the undecidability of the <a href="/wiki/Entscheidungsproblem" title="Entscheidungsproblem">Entscheidungsproblem</a> by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing" title="Alan Turing">Alan Turing</a>, and his presentation of the <a href="/wiki/Church%E2%80%93Turing_thesis" title="ChurchâTuring thesis">ChurchâTuring thesis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theory_of_computation:_formal_languages,_automata,_and_complexity_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theory_of_computation:_formal_languages,_automata,_and_complexity-142">[139]</a></sup> Today recursion theory is mostly concerned with the more refined problem of <a href="/wiki/Complexity_class" title="Complexity class">complexity classes</a> and the classification of <a href="/wiki/Turing_degree" title="Turing degree">degrees of unsolvability</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theory_of_computation:_formal_languages,_automata,_and_complexity6_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Theory_of_computation:_formal_languages,_automata,_and_complexity6-143">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Computational_logic">Computational logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Computational logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Computational_logic" title="Computational logic">Computational logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Logic_in_computer_science" title="Logic in computer science">Logic in computer science</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TransistorANDgate.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="365" decoding="async" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/TransistorANDgate.png/220px-TransistorANDgate.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/TransistorANDgate.png/330px-TransistorANDgate.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7e/TransistorANDgate.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TransistorANDgate.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Conjunction (AND) is one of the basic operations of boolean logic. It can be electronically implemented in several ways, for example, by using two <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistors</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In computer science, logic is studied as part of the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_computation" title="Theory of computation">theory of computation</a>. Key areas of logic that are relevant to computing include <a href="/wiki/Computability_theory" title="Computability theory">computability theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Category_theory" title="Category theory">category theory</a>. Early computer machinery was based on ideas from logic such as the <a href="/wiki/Lambda_calculus" title="Lambda calculus">lambda calculus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[146]</a></sup> Computer scientists also apply concepts from logic to problems in computing and vice versa. The works of <a href="/wiki/Claude_Shannon" title="Claude Shannon">Claude Shannon</a> were influential in this regard. He showed how <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boolean_logic" title="Boolean logic">boolean logic</a> can be used to understand and implement computer circuits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[148]</a></sup> The interaction between the two disciplines can be seen, for example, in how modern <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a> builds on logicians' work in <a href="/wiki/Argumentation_theory" title="Argumentation theory">argumentation theory</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Automated_theorem_proving" title="Automated theorem proving">automated theorem proving</a> can assist logicians in finding and checking proofs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[149]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Logic_programming" title="Logic programming">logic programming</a> languages such as <a href="/wiki/Prolog" title="Prolog">Prolog</a>, a program computes the consequences of logical axioms and rules to answer a query.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[150]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_semantics_of_natural_language">Formal semantics of natural language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Formal semantics of natural language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Formal_semantics_(natural_language)" title="Formal semantics (natural language)">Formal semantics (natural language)</a></div>
<p>Formal semantics, a subfield of both <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a> and philosophy, uses logic to analyze meaning in <a href="/wiki/Natural_language" title="Natural language">natural language</a>. It is an empirical field which seeks to characterize the <a href="/wiki/Denotation" title="Denotation">denotations</a> of linguistic expressions and explain how those denotations are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compositionality" title="Compositionality">composed</a> from the meanings of their parts. The field was developed by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Montague" title="Richard Montague">Richard Montague</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Partee" title="Barbara Partee">Barbara Partee</a> in the 1970s, and remains an active area of research. Central questions include <a href="/wiki/Scope_(formal_semantics)" title="Scope (formal semantics)">scope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Binding_(linguistics)" title="Binding (linguistics)">binding</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguistic_modality" title="Linguistic modality">linguistic modality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parteehndbk_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-parteehndbk-154">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology_of_logic">Epistemology of logic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Epistemology of logic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> of logic investigates how one knows that an argument is valid or that a proposition is logically true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warren2020-6_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warren2020-6-158">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schechter_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schechter-159">[156]</a></sup> This includes questions like how to justify that modus ponens is a valid rule of inference or that contradictions are false.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warren2020-6_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-Warren2020-6-158">[155]</a></sup> The traditionally dominant view is that this form of logical understanding belongs to knowledge <a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">a priori</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schechter_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schechter-159">[156]</a></sup> In this regard, it is often argued that the <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> has a special faculty to examine relations between pure ideas and that this faculty is also responsible for apprehending logical truths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GÃ³mez-Torrente2019_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-GÃ³mez-Torrente2019-160">[157]</a></sup> A similar approach understands the rules of logic in terms of <a href="/wiki/Conventionalism" title="Conventionalism">linguistic conventions</a>. On this view, the laws of logic are trivial since they are true by definition: they just express the meanings of the logical vocabulary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GÃ³mez-Torrente2019_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-GÃ³mez-Torrente2019-160">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warren2020-1_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Warren2020-1-161">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Warren2020-6_158-2"><a href="#cite_note-Warren2020-6-158">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>Important objections to the view that logic is knowable a priori were presented in the 20th century by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W._V._Quine" title="W. V. Quine">W. V. Quine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hilary_Putnam" title="Hilary Putnam">Hilary Putnam</a>. In his paper "<a href="/wiki/Is_Logic_Empirical%3F" title="Is Logic Empirical?">Is Logic Empirical?</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Is_Logic_Empirical?_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Is_Logic_Empirical?-162">[159]</a></sup> Putnam builds on a suggestion by Quine and argues that, in general, the facts of propositional logic have a similar epistemological status as facts about the physical universe. This pertains, for example, to the laws of <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a> or of <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">general relativity</a>, and in particular to what physicists have learned about quantum mechanics. According to Putnam, these insights provide a compelling case for abandoning certain familiar principles of classical logic: if one wants to be a <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_realism" title="Philosophical realism">realist</a> about the physical phenomena described by quantum theory, then one should abandon the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_distributivity" title="Principle of distributivity">principle of distributivity</a>. He suggests that classical logic be replaced with the <a href="/wiki/Quantum_logic" title="Quantum logic">quantum logic</a> proposed by <a href="/wiki/Garrett_Birkhoff" title="Garrett Birkhoff">Garrett Birkhoff</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Logic_of_Quantum_Mechanics_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Logic_of_Quantum_Mechanics-163">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_logic" title="History of logic">History of logic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2275" data-file-width="1700" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg/150px-Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg/225px-Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg/300px-Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aristotle_Altemps_Inv8575.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aristotle's logic was the canon of logic in western philosophy for over two thousand years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IEPAristotle_88-7"><a href="#cite_note-IEPAristotle-88">[85]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Logic was developed independently in several cultures during antiquity. One major early contributor was <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, who developed <a href="/wiki/Term_logic" title="Term logic">term logic</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Organon" title="Organon">Organon</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Prior_Analytics" title="Prior Analytics">Prior Analytics</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-achievement_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-achievement-164">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aristotle_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aristotle-165">[162]</a></sup> In this approach, <i>judgements</i> are broken down into <i>propositions</i> consisting of two terms that are related by one of a fixed number of relations. Inferences are expressed by means of <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogisms</a> that consist of two propositions sharing a common term as premise, and a conclusion that is a proposition involving the two unrelated terms from the premises. Aristotle's monumental insight was the notion that arguments can be characterized in terms of their form. The later logician <a href="/wiki/Jan_%C5%81ukasiewicz" title="Jan Åukasiewicz">Åukasiewicz</a> described this insight as "one of Aristotle's greatest inventions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aristotle_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aristotle-165">[162]</a></sup> Aristotle's system of logic was also responsible for the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Hypothetical_syllogism" title="Hypothetical syllogism">hypothetical syllogism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-google-166">[163]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Temporal_logic" title="Temporal logic">temporal</a> <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google1_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-google1-167">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google2_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-google2-168">[165]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive logic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-google3_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-google3-169">[166]</a></sup> as well as influential vocabulary such as <a href="/wiki/Terminology" title="Terminology">terms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Predicable" title="Predicable">predicables</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogisms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">propositions</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aristotelian_logic" title="Aristotelian logic">Aristotelian logic</a> was highly regarded in classical and medieval times, both in Europe and the Middle East. It remained in wide use in the West until the early 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mtu_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-mtu-170">[167]</a></sup> It has now been superseded by later work, though many of its key insights are still present in modern systems of logic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_(980-1037)_CIPB2067.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2692" data-file-width="1908" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg/150px-Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg/225px-Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg/300px-Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_%28980-1037%29_CIPB2067.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Avicenne_-_Avicenna_-_Ibn_Sina_(980-1037)_CIPB2067.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Avicenna's logic replaced Aristotelian logic in the <a href="/wiki/Logic_in_Islamic_philosophy" title="Logic in Islamic philosophy">Islamic discourse</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[169]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Ibn Sina</a> (Avicenna) (980â1037 CE) was the founder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avicennian_logic" title="Avicennian logic">Avicennian logic</a>, which replaced Aristotelian logic as the dominant system of <a href="/wiki/Logic_in_Islamic_philosophy" title="Logic in Islamic philosophy">logic in the Islamic world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hasse_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hasse-173">[170]</a></sup> It also had an important influence on Western medieval writers such as <a href="/wiki/Albertus_Magnus" title="Albertus Magnus">Albertus Magnus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[171]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[173]</a></sup> Ibn Sina wrote on the <a href="/wiki/Hypothetical_syllogism" title="Hypothetical syllogism">hypothetical syllogism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-177">[174]</a></sup> and on the <a href="/wiki/Propositional_calculus" title="Propositional calculus">propositional calculus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[175]</a></sup> He developed an original "temporally modalized" syllogistic theory, involving <a href="/wiki/Temporal_logic" title="Temporal logic">temporal logic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modal_logic" title="Modal logic">modal logic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica2_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica2-179">[176]</a></sup> He also made use of <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive logic</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Mill%27s_Methods" title="Mill's Methods">his methods</a> of agreement, difference, and concomitant variation, which are critical to the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodman_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goodman-177">[174]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fakhr_al-Din_al-Razi" title="Fakhr al-Din al-Razi">Fakhr al-Din al-Razi</a> (b. 1149) criticised Aristotle's "<a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">first figure</a>" and formulated an early system of inductive logic, foreshadowing the system of inductive logic developed by <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> (1806â1873).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iqbal_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iqbal-180">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_of_Ockham.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="271" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/William_of_Ockham.png/150px-William_of_Ockham.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/William_of_Ockham.png/225px-William_of_Ockham.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/70/William_of_Ockham.png 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_of_Ockham.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>William of Ockham wrote influential comments on Aristotle's logic and is well-known for his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Summa_Logicae" title="Summa Logicae">Summa Logicae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[178]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In Europe during the later medieval period, major efforts were made to show that Aristotle's ideas were compatible with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_faith" title="Christian faith">Christian faith</a>. During the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, logic became a main focus of philosophers, who would engage in critical logical analyses of philosophical arguments, often using variations of the methodology of <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">scholasticism</a>. Initially, medieval Christian scholars drew on the classics that had been preserved in Latin through commentaries by such figures such as <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka2020_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka2020-182">[179]</a></sup> Later, the work of Islamic philosophers such as <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Ibn Sina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Ibn Rushd</a> (Averroes 1126â1198 CE) were drawn on. This expanded the range of ancient works available to medieval Christian scholars since more Greek work was available to Muslim scholars that had been preserved in Latin commentaries. In 1323, <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a>'s influential <i><a href="/wiki/Sum_of_Logic" title="Sum of Logic">Summa Logicae</a></i> was released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka2020_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka2020-182">[179]</a></sup> By the 18th century, the structured approach to arguments had degenerated and fallen out of favour, as depicted in <a href="/wiki/Ludvig_Holberg" title="Ludvig Holberg">Holberg</a>'s satirical play <i><a href="/wiki/Erasmus_Montanus" title="Erasmus Montanus">Erasmus Montanus</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> criticized logic based on the claim that the logical structure of thought is a useful tool for human survival while "[l]ogic itself rests upon assumptions to which nothing in the world of reality corresponds".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[181]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/Logic_in_China" title="Logic in China">Chinese logical</a> philosopher <a href="/wiki/Gongsun_Long" title="Gongsun Long">Gongsun Long</a> (<span class="nowrap">c. 325â250 BCE</span>) proposed the paradox "One and one cannot become two, since neither becomes two". In China, the tradition of scholarly investigation into logic, however, was repressed by the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a> following the legalist philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Han_Feizi" title="Han Feizi">Han Feizi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>In India, the <a href="/wiki/Anviksiki" title="Anviksiki">Anviksiki</a> school of logic was founded by <a href="/wiki/Medh%C4%81tithi" title="MedhÄtithi">MedhÄtithi</a> (c. 6th century BCE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[183]</a></sup> Innovations in the scholastic school, called <a href="/wiki/Nyaya" title="Nyaya">Nyaya</a>, continued from ancient times into the early 18th century with the <a href="/wiki/Navya-Ny%C4%81ya" title="Navya-NyÄya">Navya-NyÄya</a> school. By the 16th century, it developed theories resembling modern logic, such as <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>'s "distinction between sense and reference of proper names" and his definition of number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[184]</a></sup> Its development of the theory of <i>restrictive conditions for universals</i> anticipated some of the developments in modern <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[iv]</a></sup> Since 1824, Indian logic attracted the attention of many Western scholars, and has had an influence on important 19th-century logicians such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Babbage" title="Charles Babbage">Charles Babbage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Augustus_De_Morgan" title="Augustus De Morgan">Augustus De Morgan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/George_Boole" title="George Boole">George Boole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Indian_logic:_a_reader_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Indian_logic:_a_reader-189">[185]</a></sup> In the 20th century, Western philosophers like <a href="/wiki/Stanislaw_Schayer" title="Stanislaw Schayer">Stanislaw Schayer</a> and Klaus Glashoff have explored Indian logic more extensively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russell,_Whitehead_-_Principia_Mathematica_to_56.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="546" data-file-width="348" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Russell%2C_Whitehead_-_Principia_Mathematica_to_56.jpg/150px-Russell%2C_Whitehead_-_Principia_Mathematica_to_56.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Russell%2C_Whitehead_-_Principia_Mathematica_to_56.jpg/225px-Russell%2C_Whitehead_-_Principia_Mathematica_to_56.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Russell%2C_Whitehead_-_Principia_Mathematica_to_56.jpg/300px-Russell%2C_Whitehead_-_Principia_Mathematica_to_56.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russell,_Whitehead_-_Principia_Mathematica_to_56.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Principia_Mathematica" title="Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a> by Alfred North Whitehead and Bertrand Russell had an important impact on the development of modern logic.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">syllogistic</a> logic developed by Aristotle predominated in the West until the mid-19th century, when interest in the <a href="/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations of mathematics</a> stimulated the development of symbolic logic (now called <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a>). In 1854, George Boole published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Laws_of_Thought" title="The Laws of Thought">The Laws of Thought</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[187]</a></sup> introducing symbolic logic and the principles of what is now known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boolean_logic" title="Boolean logic">Boolean logic</a>. In 1879, Gottlob Frege published <i><a href="/wiki/Begriffsschrift" title="Begriffsschrift">Begriffsschrift</a></i>, which inaugurated modern logic with the invention of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantification_(logic)" title="Quantification (logic)">quantifier</a> notation. This invention reconciled the Aristotelian and Stoic logics in a broader system, and solved problems for which Aristotelian logic was impotent, such as the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_multiple_generality" title="Problem of multiple generality">problem of multiple generality</a>. From 1910 to 1913, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a> published <i><a href="/wiki/Principia_Mathematica" title="Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Principia_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-Principia-138">[135]</a></sup> on the foundations of mathematics, attempting to derive mathematical truths from <a href="/wiki/Axiom" title="Axiom">axioms</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inference_rule" title="Inference rule">inference rules</a> in symbolic logic. In 1931, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6del" title="GÃ¶del">GÃ¶del</a> raised serious problems with the foundationalist program and logic ceased to focus on such issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka2020b_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka2020b-139">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>The development of logic since Frege, Russell, and Wittgenstein had a profound influence on the practice of philosophy and the perceived nature of philosophical problems (see <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic philosophy</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics" title="Philosophy of mathematics">philosophy of mathematics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hintikka2020b_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hintikka2020b-139">[136]</a></sup> Logic, especially sentential logic, is implemented in computer <a href="/wiki/Digital_electronics" title="Digital electronics">logic circuits</a> and is fundamental to <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[188]</a></sup> Logic is commonly taught by university philosophy, sociology, advertising and literature departments, often as a compulsory discipline.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Logic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-roman">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-ntvarguments-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ntvarguments_39-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Though see <a href="/wiki/Imperative_logic" title="Imperative logic">imperative logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_semantics" title="Dynamic semantics">dynamic semantics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inquisitive_semantics" title="Inquisitive semantics">inquisitive semantics</a> for logical systems which narrow or generalize the notion of valid inference to other kinds of objects.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-abduction-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-abduction_66-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On <a href="/wiki/Abductive_reasoning" title="Abductive reasoning">abductive reasoning</a>, see:

<li>Josephson, John R., and Susan G. Josephson. 1994. <i>Abductive Inference: Computation, Philosophy, Technology</i>. New York: Cambridge University Press. viii. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-521-43461-0" title="Special:BookSources/0-521-43461-0">0-521-43461-0</a>.</li>
<li>Bunt, H. and W. Black. 2000. <i>Abduction, Belief and Context in Dialogue: Studies in Computational Pragmatics</i>, (<i>Natural Language Processing</i> 1). Amsterdam: <a href="/wiki/John_Benjamins_Publishing_Company" title="John Benjamins Publishing Company">John Benjamins</a>. vi.  <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>Â <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/90-272-4983-0" title="Special:BookSources/90-272-4983-0">90-272-4983-0</a>, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-55619-794-2" title="Special:BookSources/1-55619-794-2">1-55619-794-2</a>.</li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term "a logic" is sometimes reserved for just the system's syntax, i.e. its language and proof theory. In the philosophical literature, the term is sometimes further restricted to refer only to particular logic-based formal systems such as those which are complete or motivated by intuitions close to those which motivated classical logic.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-188"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-188">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chakrabarti, Kisor Kumar. 1976. "Some Comparisons Between Frege's Logic and Navya-Nyaya Logic." <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophy_and_Phenomenological_Research" title="Philosophy and Phenomenological Research">Philosophy and Phenomenological Research</a></i> 36(4):554â63. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F2106873" rel="nofollow">10.2307/2106873</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a>Â <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/2106873" rel="nofollow">2106873</a>.

"This paper consists of three parts. The first part deals with Frege's distinction between sense and reference of proper names and a similar distinction in Navya-Nyaya logic. In the second part we have compared Frege's definition of number to the Navya-Nyaya definition of number. In the third part we have shown how the study of the so-called 'restrictive conditions for universals' in Navya-Nyaya logic anticipated some of the developments of modern set theory."</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">11 March</span> 2021</span>. <q>The two most important types of logical calculi are propositional (or sentential) calculi and functional (or predicate) calculi. A propositional calculus is a system containing propositional variables and connectives (some also contain propositional constants) but not individual or functional variables or constants. In the extended propositional calculus, quantifiers whose operator variables are propositional variables are added.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopedia+of+philosophy&amp;rft.place=Detroit&amp;rft.pages=535-536&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Thomson+Gale%2FMacmillan+Reference+USA&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F61151356&amp;rft.isbn=0-02-865780-2&amp;rft.aulast=Brody&amp;rft.aufirst=Boruch+A.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.worldcat.org%2Foclc%2F61151356&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALogic"></span></span>

<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation cs2"><a class="external text" href="https://www.encyclopediaofmath.org/index.php?title=Logical_calculus" rel="nofollow">"Logical calculus"</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia_of_Mathematics" title="Encyclopedia of Mathematics">Encyclopedia of Mathematics</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/European_Mathematical_Society" title="European Mathematical Society">EMS Press</a>, 2001 [1994]</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Logical+calculus&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopedia+of+Mathematics&amp;rft.pub=EMS+Press&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.encyclopediaofmath.org%2Findex.php%3Ftitle%3DLogical_calculus&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALogic"></span></li>
<li>Introductions and tutorials
<ul><li><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080403145334/http://www.galilean-library.org/manuscript.php?postid=43782" rel="nofollow">"An Introduction to Philosophical Logic, by Paul Newall"</a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://www.galilean-library.org/manuscript.php?postid=43782" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 3 April 2008.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=An+Introduction+to+Philosophical+Logic%2C+by+Paul+Newall&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.galilean-library.org%2Fmanuscript.php%3Fpostid%3D43782&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALogic"></span> aimed at beginners.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.fecundity.com/logic/" rel="nofollow">forall x: an introduction to formal logic</a>, by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=P.D._Magnus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="P.D. Magnus (page does not exist)">P.D. Magnus</a>, covers sentential and quantified logic.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080521095350/http://www.filozofia.uw.edu.pl/kpaprzycka/Publ/xLogicSelfTaught.html" rel="nofollow">Logic Self-Taught: A Workbook</a> (originally prepared for on-line logic instruction).</li></ul></li>
<li>Essays
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/28696" rel="nofollow">"Symbolic Logic"</a> and <a class="external text" href="https://www.gutenberg.org/ebooks/4763" rel="nofollow">"The Game of Logic"</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll">Lewis Carroll</a>, 1896.</li></ul></li>
<li>Online Tools
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150515000453/http://www.thefirstscience.org/syllogistic-machine" rel="nofollow">Interactive Syllogistic Machine</a> A web-based syllogistic machine for exploring fallacies, figures, terms, and modes of syllogisms.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://somerby.net/mack/logic/" rel="nofollow">A Logic Calculator</a> A web-based application for evaluating simple statements in symbolic logic.</li></ul></li>
<li>Reference material
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="http://legacy.earlham.edu/~peters/courses/log/transtip.htm" rel="nofollow">Translation Tips</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Peter_Suber" title="Peter Suber">Peter Suber</a>, for translating from English into logical notation.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.ontology.co/history-of-logic.htm" rel="nofollow">Ontology and History of Logic. An Introduction</a> with an annotated bibliography.</li></ul></li></ul>
